      A S EMI -S UPERVISED C LUSTERING A PPROACH                                                             F OR
002   G RAPH L EARNING WITH N EURAL N ETWORKS

008
009                                                   A BSTRACT
010
011            We propose a semi-supervised approach that combines any unsupervised clustering ob-
012            jective and supervised objective for end-to-end training any neural networks to improve
013            node classification in attributed graphs, particularly when training labels are sparse. Our
014            framework formulates node classification as semi-supervised inference of neural network
015            models of attributed graphs with cluster structure. We use this framework to understand
016            how neural networks for graph clustering can jointly cluster node attributes and graph
017            structure, despite graph clustering objectives explicitly considering only graph structure
018            and cluster assignments. Our framework also enables neural network architectures such
019
               as transformers and multilayer perceptrons to learn on graphs without positional encod-
               ings and without spectral or message passing layers found in graph neural networks. We
020
               evaluate our framework on six real-world attributed graph datasets.
021
022
023
      1     I NTRODUCTION
024
025
      In graph learning, a common task is to identify groups of similar nodes, known as classes or clusters (Wu
026
      et al., 2022; Liu et al., 2023; Daneshfar et al., 2024). In a social network, for example, where connections
027
      between people are represented as edges in a graph, node classes or clusters can represent groups of people
028   that are members of the same community (Bedi & Sharma, 2016). Graphs can also have attributes asso-
029   ciated with each node, such as the age and dietary preferences of each person in a social network. Many
030   different notions of communities in the same social network can be considered meaningful, and how the both
031   attributes of individuals and their connections in the network give rise to these communities can be complex.
032   Analogously, many different clusterings of the same attributed graph can be considered meaningful, and
033   how both the attributes of nodes and their adjacencies give rise to these clusterings can be complex. Graph
034   neural networks (GNNs) (Wu et al., 2022; Corso et al., 2024) are artificial neural networks designed to learn
035   complex functions of node attributes and adjacencies. Where sufficient training labels are available, GNNs
036
      can be trained supervised and end-to-end for node classification (Kipf & Welling, 2016a). When no training
      labels are available, GNNs have recently been adapted for end-to-end unsupervised clustering (Shchur &
037
      Günnemann, 2019; Tsitsulin et al., 2023; Blöcker et al., 2024). However, unsupervised clustering may yield
038
      cluster assignments that do not align with the node labels of interest in real-world graphs. While it can be
039   prohibitively expensive to obtain sufficient training labels for supervised node classification, it can be prac-
040   tical to label just a handful of examples for each label. In this work we propose a semi-supervised approach
041   that combines unsupervised clustering objectives and supervised objectives for training neural networks to
042   improve node classification in attributed graphs, particularly when training labels are sparse.
043
044   1.1   R ESEARCH G APS AND C ONTRIBUTIONS
045
046   We summarize the following research gaps we identify and our contributions to each.


                                                         1
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


047
048
049
050
051
052
053
054
055
056
057
      Figure 1: Visualization of node labellings comparing an unsupervised clustering, a semi-supervised node
058
      classification, and a semi-supervised clustering of the Coauthor CS dataset (Shchur et al., 2018), selected
059   among 10 runs of random splits of 2 training nodes per cluster, 50 validation nodes, and 1000 test nodes.
060   The unsupervised clustering labelling was selected from the run and configuration with the lowest training
061   loss. The supervised classification and supervised clustering labellings were selected from the runs that
062   yielded the highest validation set Matthews correlation coefficient (MCC). MCCs for each node labelling is
063   displayed in parentheses.
064
065
066   Research Gap 1: While self-supervised deep graph representation learning methods (Veličković et al., 2019;
067   Hassani & Khasahmadi, 2020; Zhu et al., 2020; Devvrit et al., 2022; Thakoor et al., 2022) have enabled
068   deep graph clustering (Wang et al., 2023; Liu et al., 2023) with a two-step approach, Tsitsulin et al. (2023)
069   recently identified a research gap in graph neural networks for end-to-end unsupervised graph clustering.
070   They proposed a method to bridge the gap between traditional graph clustering objectives and graph neural
071   networks, upon which other methods (Blöcker et al., 2024; Hansen & Bianchi, 2023) have been proposed.
072   We identify a similar research gap, also identified by a recent review (Daneshfar et al., 2024), in graph neural
073
      networks for end-to-end semi-supervised graph clustering.
074   Contribution 1: We propose a reformulation of semi-supervised node classification on attributed graphs
075   in the very sparse label setting as semi-supervised attributed graph clustering. Our experiments show that
076   training with semi-supervised objectives consistently outperform a purely supervised objective. Figure 1
077   visualizes our approach on a real-world attributed graph dataset.
078   Research Gap 2: In unsupervised clustering on real-world attributed graphs, the loss landscape of clusters
079   can be complex with many candidate solutions that can be considered meaningful (Peixoto, 2021; Blöcker
080   & Scholtes, 2024).
081
      Contribution 2: We propose a semi-supervised framework for evaluating existing end-to-end unsupervised
082
      deep graph clustering methods such as NOCD (Shchur & Günnemann, 2019), DMoN (Tsitsulin et al., 2023),
083   Neuromap (Blöcker et al., 2024), and contribute an initial evaluation of these methods on six real-world
084   attributed graph datasets. We additionally contribute a fully neural stochastic block model which we evaluate
085   against.
086
      Research Gap 3: We identify a contradiction where attributed graph clustering methods such as NOCD
087
      (Shchur & Günnemann, 2019), DMoN (Tsitsulin et al., 2023), Neuromap (Blöcker et al., 2024) optimize ob-
088
      jectives that are functions of cluster assignments and graph structure, but not of node attributes. Constructing
089   Bayesian network representations of their conditional dependencies, we identify a further contradiction that
090   the explicit or implicit generative models that these methods infer are only generative models of graph struc-
091   ture and not of node attributes. On the other hand, we observe that MLPs have been shown effectively
092   optimize unsupervised graph clustering objectives end-to-end (Shchur & Günnemann, 2019; Blöcker et al.,
093   2024).

                                                         2
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


094
      Contribution 3: We propose an alternative answer to the question posed in Shchur & Günnemann (2019):
095
      “Do we really need a graph neural network?” by proposing two simple ways existing methods can be
096   adapted to learn explicit or implicit generative models both of graph structure and node attributes. If graph
097   neural networks are used to optimize graph clustering objectives, an additional neural network module can
098   be integrated to generate node attributes form cluster assignments. Alternatively, optimizing graph clustering
099   objectives with neural networks of only node attributes learns a generative model akin to the neural-prior
100   stochastic block model (Duranthon & Zdeborová, 2023b), a recently proposed generative model of graphs
101   with cluster structure from node attributes.
102   Research Gap 4: Adapting other neural networks such as multilayer perceptrons and transformers (Vaswani
103   et al., 2017) typically involve incorporating a combination of the spectral and/or message passing layers
104   found in graph neural networks (Chen et al., 2021; Rampasek et al., 2022; Qarkaxhija et al., 2024; Buterez
105   et al., 2024) and/or additional encodings informative of graph structure (Ying et al., 2021; Kim et al., 2022;
106   Ma et al., 2023; Huang et al., 2024).
107
      Contribution 4: We propose an alternative approach for neural networks such as transformers and multi-
108   layer perceptrons to learn on attributed graphs without positional or structural encodings and without spec-
109   tral or message passing layers found in graph neural networks. We evaluate this ability of the framework
110   on real-world attributed graph datasets and demonstrate that it improves learning on architectures such as
111   transformers and MLPs.
112
113
      2     S EMI -S UPERVISED D EEP G RAPH C LUSTERING E ND - TO -E ND
114
115
      Setting: Consider a node-attributed graph G := (V, E) of n nodes with node set V and edge set E.
116
      Nodes have attributes captured in a node attribute matrix X and edges (equivalently, node adjacencies) are
117   represented by an adjacency matrix A. Each node is additionally assigned to one or multiple clusters from
118   a set S of cardinality dS of which (dS )obs ≤ dS are observed. The node cluster assignments are represented
119   with a label matrix S of shape n × dS . Nodes and clusters are indexed with zero-based integers. Without
120   loss of generality it is assumed in this work that the labels of the first s nodes belonging to the first (dS )obs
121   clusters are observed, which can be used for training. S is defined in generality to allow for various forms of
122   cluster assignments, such as hard or soft assignments (Yu et al., 2005), and overlapping or non-overlapping
123   clusters (Shchur & Günnemann, 2019). The primary learning task of interest is to infer the node label matrix
124   S. We primarily consider the setting of sparse training labels, i.e. s ≪ n, though our proposed framework
125
      proposed does not rely on this assumption.
126   The formulations in this section primarily consider unweighted and undirected graphs without edge and
127   graph attributes for both brevity and to match the setting of the experiments on real-world attributed graph
128   datasets that are also unweighted and undirected. In appendix B.1 we discuss extensions to weighted and
129   directed graphs with edge and graph attributes.
130
131   2.1     G RAPH N EURAL N ETWORKS WITHOUT Attributed G RAPH G ENERATIVE M ODELS
132
133   Our first contribution is to identify the generative models that graph neural networks for semi-supervised
134
      node classification and unsupervised graph clustering infer. By constructing their Bayesian network rep-
      resentations, we show how these graph neural networks should not learn generative models of (node-
135
      )attributed graphs.
136
137
      2.1.1    E XPLICIT AND Implicit G ENERATIVE M ODELS
138
139   Graph clustering functions such as NOCD (Shchur & Günnemann, 2019), DMoN (Tsitsulin et al., 2023),
140   Neuromap (Blöcker et al., 2024) and graph pooling functions such as MinCutPool (Bianchi et al., 2020) and

155
      models of modularity maximization and Infomap optimizing the map equation (Smiljanić et al., 2023) – the
156
      discrete combinatorial optimization objectives relaxed by DMoN and Neuromap, respectively – and their
157   equivalence to stochastic block models have been shown by Peixoto & Kirkley (2022). We hypothesise
158   that this can be extended to the graph clustering functions of DMoN, Neuromap, and similar methods, and
159   empirically evaluate this in our experiments.

      2.2   C ONDITIONAL D EPENDENCIES OF ATTRIBUTED G RAPH G ENERATIVE M ODELS WITH C LUSTER
189
            S TRUCTURE
190
191   To construct models that learn generative models of attributed graphs with cluster structure, we make the
192   contribution of identifying the following requirements of node-attributed generative models of graphs with
193   cluster structure and their representations as Bayesian networks:
194
195           • Ab depends on S,
                               b represented by directed edge from S
                                                                   b to Ab in a Bayesian network.
196           • Either X
                       c depends on Sb or S
                                          b depends on X, represented by the existence of a directed edge between
197             them in a Bayesian network.
198
199
              • Conditional independence between X (or X) c and A b given S.
                                                                          b
200
      Requiring conditional independence between X (or X)   c and A   b given S
                                                                              b ensures that the cluster structure of
201
      the attributed graph contains all the necessary information to infer both node attributes and graph structure.
202
203   We identify two generative models of attributed graphs with cluster structure that satisfy the conditions pro-
204   posed above – the neural-prior stochastic block model (Duranthon & Zdeborová, 2023b) and the contextual
205   stochastic block model (Deshpande et al., 2018) – as evidenced by their Bayesian network representations in
206
      figure 3 in appendix A. In the following sections, we propose fully neural network variants of these models,
      the NSBM and the CSBM, respectively.
207

243   Using the NSBM, we contribute a framework for neural networks such as transformers and MLPs to learn
244   on attributed graphs without positional or structural encodings (Ying et al., 2021; Kim et al., 2022; Ma et al.,
245   2023; Huang et al., 2024) and without spectral or message passing layers found in graph neural networks
246   (Chen et al., 2021; Rampasek et al., 2022; Qarkaxhija et al., 2024; Buterez et al., 2024).
247

276   2.4.1    R ECONSTRUCTING ATTRIBUTES L EARNS G ENERATIVE M ODELS OF ATTRIBUTED G RAPHS
277
278   Like the NSBM, the CBAE is also trained end-to-end by optimizing the reconstruction losses of its predic-
279   tions.

280

281


                                                         6
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


282
      Depending on the attributes observed, the attribute reconstruction objective LV can a combination of re-
283
      gression objectives such as mean squared error or mean absolute error, and classification objectives such
284   as cross-entropy. As we identified in section 2.1.1, without the attribute reconstruction we propose for
285   the CBAE, graph neural networks for unsupervised graph clustering should learn generative models only
286   of graph structure. This highlights the importance of attribute reconstruction for learning on graphs with
287   attributes with graph neural networks.
288
289   2.5   R ECONSTRUCTING S EMI -S UPERVISED N ODE C LASSIFICATION
290
291   By reinterpreting the reconstruction objectives of the NSBM (equation 5) and CBAE (equation 8) as semi-
292   supervised clustering objectives, we contribute a framework where any neural network or graph neural net-
293
      work can utilize any unsupervised clustering objective, reconstruction objective, or self-supervised objective
      to learn on attributed graphs with cluster structure in a semi-supervised manner.
294
295                         lNSBM := Lsupervised Sb0:s,:(d ) , S0:s,:(d )      + (Lunsupervised )NSBM            (9)
                                                          S obs        S obs
296
297                         lCBAE := Lsupervised Sb0:s,:(d ) , S0:s,:(d )
                                                          S obs        S obs
                                                                               + (Lunsupervised )CBAE           (10)
298
299
      where LS = Lsupervised is a supervised classification objective such as cross-entropy (see appendix A), and
300                                  (Lunsupervised )NSBM = LE + Lregularization
301                                  (Lunsupervised )CBAE = LE + LV + Lregularization
302
      are unsupervised objectives. As we constructed in section 2.1.1, LE can be replaced with any unsupervised
303
      clustering objective such NOCD, DMoN, Neuromap, and similar methods, and Lregularization is an optional
304   regularization term such as in DiffPool, MinCutPool, and DMoN (see appendix A and E.4.1).
305
306   While we formulate semi-supervised node classification and semi-supervised graph clustering equivalently
307
      in setting of our proposed methodology, we propose two distinctions between the two: by the methods used
      to solve the problem, and by the sparsity of the labels. For semi-supervised node classification training
308
      losses are purely supervised while for semi-supervised graph clustering training loss combine supervised
309
      and unsupervised objectives. In our experiments (section 3), we explore the two label sparsity settings,
310   one with labels designed to be sufficient for supervised training, and one with labels designed to be sparse
311   for semi-supervised training where semi-supervised graph clustering objectives should outperform purely
312   supervised objectives. By constructing this semi-supervised framework we contribute (1) a way for any
313   unsupervised clustering objective, reconstruction objective, or self-supervised objective to be used along-
314   side any supervised objective to improve the performance of any neural network or graph neural network
315   on node classification, particularly when labels are sparse, and (2) a way to evaluate the performance of
316   graph clustering objectives on real-world attributed graph datasets in the setting of semi-supervised node
317   classification.
318
319   3     E XPERIMENTS
320
321   In our experiments we contribute (1) an evaluation of our proposed NSBM and CBAE in the setting of semi-
322   supervised attributed graph clustering to improve the performance of any neural network or graph neural
323   network on multi-class node classification. (2) a framework for evaluating the performance of attributed
324   graph clustering objectives in the setting of semi-supervised node classification and an initial benchmark of
325   existing attributed graph clustering methods adapted to end-to-end semi-supervised attributed graph cluster-
326
      ing.
327   Our experiments focus on adapting multi-class classification to semi-supervised graph clustering on 6 real-
328   world attributed graph datasets – Coauthor CS, Coauthor Physics, Amazon Computers, and Amazon Photo

                                                        7
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


329
      from Shchur et al. (2018), and roman-empire and amazon-ratings from Platonov et al. (2023b).
330
      The cardinality of the set of labels dS is known and a non-zero number of training nodes per label is given.
331
332   To adapt these datasets to a semi-supervised clustering, we sparsify the labels by randomly selecting a subset
333   of labels to be observed and the rest to be unobserved. Specifically, for each prescribed training, validation,
334
      and test split for each dataset, we randomly select 2 training nodes per label from the training split, and 50
      validation nodes from the validation split. The test split remains the same as the original split.
335
336   Following the proposed approach in equation 9, semi-supervised clustering objectives combine the super-
337   vised cross-entropy objective LS in equation 16 with the unsupervised objectives LE , Lregularization , and LV
338   in equation 17. We evaluate the performance of 4 unsupervised graph clustering objectives: NOCD (Shchur
339   & Günnemann, 2019), DMoN (Tsitsulin et al., 2023), Neuromap (Blöcker et al., 2024), and our proposed
340
      SBMNN , and 2 unsupervised attribute reconstruction objectives: DMoN (Tsitsulin et al., 2023) and (unit) L2
      regularization. Shchur & Günnemann (2019) showed that L2 regularization was beneficial, and we evaluate
341
      the case of unit L2 regularization which corresponds to an exact variational and description length objective
342
      (Graves, 2011), motivated by the interpretation of these model as generative models. LV is mean squared
343   error loss. The performance of semi-supervised objectives over purely supervised objectives is evaluated on
344   4 neural network architectures, two graph neural networks – GCN (Kipf & Welling, 2016a) and GraphSAGE
345   (Hamilton et al., 2017) – and two “non-graph” neural networks – MLP and Transformer.
346
      Our main results are summarized in table 1 for label sparsified versions of the datasets, and in appendix F.2
347
      for the default splits of the datasets. Semi-supervised objectives consistently outperform a purely supervised
348
      objective on label sparsified versions of the datasets and the performance of semi-supervised objectives over
349   purely supervised objectives is significantly more pronounced when labels are sparse. Figure 1 and the
350   additional visualizations in appendix F.1 support these findings. The best performing graph neural network
351   on 3 of 6 datasets use the attribute reconstruction in our CBAE approach, also significantly more pronounced
352   when labels are sparse (see appendix F.3 for comparing attribute reconstruction on default splits of the
353   datasets).
354
      Though graph neural networks such as GCN and GraphSAGE consistently produce the best performance
355   on these datasets, transformers and MLPs trained with semi-supervised objectives under our NSBM frame-
356   work both outperform a purely supervised objective on most datasets and perform competitively with graph
357   neural networks on some datasets such as the Coauthor CS dataset. MLPs perform consistently better than
358   transformers on these datasets. Results from Tsitsulin et al. (2023) and Blöcker et al. (2024) support these
359   findings for unsupervised graph clustering showing that on datasets where k-means node clustering (only)
360   on features outperforms SBM inference or Infomap (only) on the graph structure, MLPs outperformed graph
361   neural networks. It has also been empirically observed and studied theoretically how graph neural networks
362   use graphs when detrimentally in cases where graph structure should be ignored in favour of attributes
363
      (Bechler-Speicher et al., 2024).
364   Across architectures, the unsupervised graph clustering and regularization objectives of DMoN produced
365   the best performance on 4 of 6 datasets, while transformers consistently benefited most from unit L2 reg-
366   ularization. Additional results in appendix F.4 echo that the SBMNN function we designed and NOCD did
367   not perform as well as implicit objectives such as DMoN. While we highlight the importance of designing
368   architectures by uncovering and analysing their conditional dependencies, implicit models such as DMoN
369
      could provide a more expressive for learning on graphs. Notably we show that unsupervised graph clustering
      objectives with regularization outperform regularization alone, ablating the effect of regularization. Despite
370
      the small number of validation nodes in the sparse label setting, early stopping and model selection on the
371
      validation set still produces the best performance on the test set.
372
373   In the appendix, we include additional experimental details and results and discuss possible extensions of
374   our framework to settings such as an unknown number of labels as well as unbalanced and unseen labels.
375

449
450
451
      4     C ONCLUSION
452
453
454   In this work we propose and evaluate a semi-supervised clustering approach for graph learning with neural
455   networks. Our framework combines enables any unsupervised graph clustering objective to improve the
456
      performance of any neural network or graph neural network on multi-class node classification in the setting
      of semi-supervised graph clustering. The perspective we introduce helps us to understand how current graph
457
      neural network methods for graph clustering jointly cluster node attributes and graph structure, despite
458
      clustering objectives explicitly considering only node attributes and graph structure. Our framework also
459   introduces an alternative graph learning approach, enabling such as neural networks such as transformers and
460   multi-layer perceptrons to learn on graphs without positional or structural encodings and without spectral
461   or message passing layers found in graph neural networks. The results of our experiments demonstrate that
462   our approach is complementary to existing semi-supervised node classification approaches on six real-world
463   node-attributed graph datasets, improving learning when training labels are sparse.
464
      We propose that the adaptation evaluation of our approach for link prediction and other edge-level tasks,
465   extending the approach for edge and graph-level learning tasks, and further investigating the impact of
466   attribute reconstruction for graph neural network methods for graph clustering could be interesting avenues
467   for future work. Theoretical analysis of the expressivity of the alternative graph learning approach for non-
468   graph neural networks is also an open question which we hope to explore with the community. We discuss
469   potential future work further in appendix B.2.

                                                         10
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

      5   R EPRODUCIBILITY S TATEMENT
471
472
      All source code and documentation to reproduce results is made available in the supplementary zip file. All
473
      dataset sources are detailed in the appendix, and the source code includes the necessary code to fetch the
474   datasets. Experiments were run on a cluster with virtual machines each with 16 vCPUs and 128 GB RAM, a
475   single NVIDIA L40 GPU with 48 GB VRAM, and 100 GB disk space. The presented experiments require
476   approximately 1 year on a single virtual machine of the cluster to run, and the source code is designed to be
477   run on a cluster with multiple virtual machines in parallel.

831   A     A DDITIONAL BACKGROUND
832
833   In this section we provide additional background on the neural-prior stochastic block model, the contex-
834   tual stochastic block model, Bayesian networks, and end-to-end semi-supervised node classification and
835
      unsupervised graph clustering with graph neural networks.
836
837   A.1   N ODE -ATTRIBUTED S TOCHASTIC B LOCK M ODELS
838
      Attributed graphs with cluster structure can be generated by attributed variants of a class of random graph
839   models called stochastic block models. Stochastic block models (SBMs) are generative models of random
840
      graphs with cluster structure. A prediction A b of A is generated from predicted assignments of nodes to
841
      clusters S,
               b and a block matrix B, which can be fixed or inferred from data.
842
843

844                                              b ∼ SBM S,
                                                 A        b B                                               (11)
845


                                                      18
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


846
      Each element Ba,b of the block matrix defines the probability that a node in cluster a connects to a node in
847
      cluster b. A prediction of S
                                 b of S and, if learned, B can be inferred by minimizing the reconstruction error
848
849
      between A  b and A. B and other variables that are considered never directed observed are always written in
      this work without a b .
850
851   One variant of the SBM can be constructed such that each edge A   bi,j ∼ Bernoulli (Wi,j ) is drawn indepen-
852   dently element-wise from a Bernoulli distribution
853
                          Ab ∼ SBMB S,  b B := Bernoulli (W ) := Bernoulli σ SB     b S  bT                   (12)
854
855
      where σ is an activation function applied element-wise that ensures, when necessary, that W is a matrix of
856   valid edge probabilities e.g. sigmoid. For the case when S is normalized such that cluster assignments for
857   each node sums to 1, σ can simply be an identity map.
858
859
      This work builds on two node-attributed variants of the SBM, the neural-prior stochastic block model (Du-
      ranthon & Zdeborová, 2023b) and the contextual stochastic block model (Deshpande et al., 2018).
860
861

864   neural-prior stochastic block model, which can be expressed as

      A.2     G ENERATIVE M ODELS AS BAYESIAN N ETWORKS
884   The dependence of variables in generative models such as the stochastic block model (SBM) and two of
885   its node-attributed variants, NSBMo and CSBMo can be visualized as directed acyclic graphical models –
886   Bayesian networks – as shown in figure 2. Bayesian networks (Kitson et al., 2023) can be helpful to visualize
887   conditional dependencies and are used in section 2 to build upon the generative models introduced in this
888   section. A directed edge from a variable a to a variable b in a Bayesian network represents a dependence of b
889   on a. The Bayesian networks in figure 3 show that NSBMo and CSBMo are two possible Bayesian networks
890   that generate graph structures A
                                     b from clusters Sb and with A and X (or X) c conditionally independent given
891   S. There are, however, other generative models connecting X and A (or their predictions) and S,
       b                                                                                                 b such as
892   graph neural networks for (semi-supervised) node classification.

      A.4    U NSUPERVISED C LUSTERING
928
929   In the case where S is not observed, S can be predicted by inferring a stochastic block model using graph
930   neural networks. Graph neural networks for unsupervised clustering can be written as neural network func-
931   tions identical to equation 15, while distinguished from node classification models by their training with
932   unsupervised loss functions. This work proposes that unsupervised losses can be functions of S
                                                                                                   b and, option-
933   ally, any combination of X and A such that
                                  c         b

938   Pool  (Bianchi

      For example, DiffPool employs “entropy regularization” Lregularization := − n1 i=1 a=1      Si,a log (Si,a )

      to encourage cluster assignments that are close to one-hot vectors, and reconstructs A as A   b := S  bSbT ,

944
      interpreted as a reconstruction negative log-likelihood of an SBM with weighted edges and an identity block
      matrix B = I.
945
946   Extending the perspective of Peixoto & Kirkley (2022), other clustering approaches including spectral clus-
947   tering (MinCutPool (Bianchi et al., 2020)) and modularity (DMoN (Tsitsulin et al., 2023)) can be interpreted
948   as also inferring an implicit stochastic block model.
949   NOCD (Shchur & Günnemann, 2019) employs a “Bernoulli-Poisson” model, equivalent to variants of ran-
950   dom dot product graphs and latent space models (Athreya et al., 2018), and a special case of the SBMB
951   formulation in equation 12 with a fixed identity block matrix B = I and a custom activation

957   entropy between the set of sampled edges Esampled := E+ ∪ E− and their corresponding probabilities in W ,

      subset of entries of W . This work adapts this loss for generative models later proposed, as described in the
965
      section 2.

973
      B     E XTENDING THE F RAMEWORK
974
975
      In this section we describe extensions of our proposed framework to model other graph types and discuss
976   potential extensions to other graph learning tasks.
977
978
      B.1     G RAPH T YPES
979
980   Our proposed framework can be extended to model other graph types such as weighted graphs, directed
981   graphs, and graphs with edge and graph attributes.
982
983   B.1.1    W EIGHTED G RAPHS
984
985   To model weighted graphs, a different distribution SBM S,
                                                              b B =P
                                                                         W →A b (W ) (and corresponding recon-
986   struction loss LE ) modelling continuous edge weights can be used (instead of the Bernoulli distribution in

                                                       21
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


987
       equation 12). For example, for a Gaussian distribution with zero mean and unit variance, the reconstruction
988
       loss can be the mean squared error between A  b and A. For stochastic generation of A,
                                                                                            b Sb (and, optionally,
989
       B) can be generated with a stochastic neural network, e.g. with (Monte Carlo) dropout (Gal & Ghahramani,
990
       2016) (see appendix B.2.3 for details).
991
992
993    B.1.2   D IRECTED G RAPHS
994
995    An SBM can be made directed if its block matrix is directed, which can be fixed or learned. The following
996    subsection extending the framework to edge and graph attributes describes how a directed block matrix can
997
       be learned for the proposed NSBM and CSBM.
998
999    B.1.3   E DGE AND G RAPH ATTRIBUTES
1000
1001   To model node attributes XV , edge attributes XE , and graph attributes XG , the NSBM and CSBM we
1002   propose can be extended as follows. The graph neural network encoder of the autoencoders can be a neural
1003   network that is able to learn with edge attributes such as SAT (Chen et al., 2022), and a global pooling
1004   operation such as sum, mean, or max pooling can be incorporated to produce graph attributes.
1005
       U can be interpreted as analogous to the node attributes in the NSBM and CSBM. A
                                                                                      b and X or (X)
                                                                                                  c are
1006
       conditionally independent given S but conditionally dependent given XE .
                                       b                                   b

1070   B.2     OTHER G RAPH L EARNING TASKS
1071
       Here we discuss potential extensions of our proposed framework to other graph learning tasks such as semi-
1072
       supervised graph clustering with unseen and unbalanced clusters, edge and graph-level tasks, and stochastic
1073
       generation of attributed graphs with cluster structure.
1074
1075
       B.2.1    U NSEEN AND U NBALANCED C LUSTERS
1076
1077   When unknown, the cardinality dS of the set of node labels S can be learned by      √ setting an upper bound
1078   estimate (dS )max ≥ (dS )obs . One heuristic for choosing this is upper bound is n, found from empirical
1079   studies of real-world graphs (Ghasemian et al., 2019). The first (dS )obs rows of S
                                                                                         b can be trained to align with
1080   supervised training labels, while the remaining (dS )max −(dS )obs learn unseen labels. Implicit regularization


                                                                  23
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1081
       of neural networks (Razin et al., 2022) and optional explicit regularization can learn compressed represen-
1082
       tations, learning a predicted dc
                                      S < (dS )max as demonstrated in the unsupervised clustering experiments in
1083
       Blöcker et al. (2024).
1084
1085   Methods such as NOCD (Shchur & Günnemann, 2019), Neuromap (Blöcker et al., 2024), and the SBMNN
1086   we propose avoid assuming a balanced clustering, and can be used in place of methods such as MinCutPool
1087   (Bianchi et al., 2020), DMoN (Tsitsulin et al., 2023), and ACCPool (Hansen & Bianchi, 2023), in the setting
1088
       of unbalanced clusters.
1089
1090
       B.2.2   E DGE AND G RAPH -L EVEL TASKS
1091
1092
       The graph structure reconstruction objectives we study and propose in our framework is compatible with
1093
       common link prediction objectives (see appendix A). The extension of our framework to link prediction is
1094   straightforward, as the reconstruction loss LE can be replaced with a link prediction loss such as the binary
1095   cross entropy loss in equation 19.
1096
1097
       The extensions to model edge and graph attributes we discussed in the previous section can be used adapt the
       framework to edge and graph-level tasks such as edge classification and regression, and graph classification
1098
       and regression by optimizing their respective reconstruction losses.
1099
1100
1101   B.2.3   S TOCHASTIC G ENERATION OF ATTRIBUTE G RAPHS WITH C LUSTER S TRUCTURE
1102
1103   While we study and develop our framework using common neural network that are designed to be determin-
1104   istic at test time, the proposed NSBM and CBAE generative models can be extended to sample from learned
1105   distributions at test time. As all neural networks in our experiments were trained with dropout, Monte Carlo
1106   dropout (Gal & Ghahramani, 2016) can be used to sample from the learned distribution at test time.
1107
       The extensions to edge and graph attributes we discussed in the previous section (appendix B.1) can be used
1108   to adapt the NSBM and CSBM to generate graphs with edge and graph attributes.
1109
1110   Our framework is also designed to be compatible with other deep generative models (Zhu et al., 2022), and
1111
       the NSBM and CBAE with objectives such as variational learning, normalizing flows, and score matching
       can be used to learn and generate attributed graphs with cluster structure.
1112
1113
1114
1115   C    S CALABILITY AND C OMPUTATIONAL C OMPLEXITY
1116
1117   Due to the generality of our proposed framework, the computational complexity of the proposed NSBM and
1118   CSBM and their respective autoencoders is not fixed, but depends on the neural network modules, clustering
1119   methods, and regularization methods used. For example, if prototypical transformers with quadratic com-
1120   plexity in the number of nodes are used as neural networks of the NSBM, the computational complexity of
1121   the NSBM will also be quadratic in the number of nodes. However, if an MLP is used, the computational
1122   complexity of the NSBM will be linear in the number of nodes, unless a clustering method of quadratic
1123   complexity such as DiffPool (Ying et al., 2018) is used.
1124   While we primarily focus on the full-batch setting, which is unamenable to large-scale graphs, the proposed
1125   framework can in principle be extended to allow mini-batching by using methods such as Lim et al. (2021)
1126   and by combining any (graph) neural network and clustering method that allows for mini-batching. We leave
1127   the exploration of mini-batching for future work.


                                                        24
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1128
       D     OTHER R ELATED W ORK
1129
1130
1131
       D.1   S EMI -S UPERVISED N ODE C LASSIFICATION
1132
       Semi-supervised node classification (Kipf & Welling, 2016a) studies the problem of classifying nodes in
1133
       the setting of sparse training labels. The inductive bias provided by the observed graph can be effectively
1134
       utilized by methods such as label propagation (Xiaojin & Zoubin, 2002; Li et al., 2019; Lin et al., 2020;
1135   Rossi et al., 2022).
1136
1137   Since the seminal work of semi-supervised node classification with graph convolutional networks (Kipf &
1138
       Welling, 2016a), several later works study a setting of sparser training labels. Li et al. (2019) combines
       label propagation and graph filters proposing a “generalized label propagation“ framework, while Lin et al.
1139
       (2020) studied how metric learning can improve the performance for graph convolutional networks and
1140
       label propagation frameworks. Several other later works adopt the perspective of Bayesian inference, from
1141   contrastive learning (Wan et al., 2021) to Bayesian graph neural networks (Pal et al., 2020), while others
1142   such as Dai et al. (2021) consider a similar problem of noisy labels.
1143
1144
       This work provides an alternative and complementary approach to semi-supervised classification with sparse
       training labels, compatible with any end-to-end neural network architecture.
1145
1146
1147   D.2   G ENERATIVE M ODELS OF ATTRIBUTED G RAPHS WITH C LUSTER S TRUCTURE
1148
1149   Generative models of graphs with cluster structure and their inference methods can be broadly categorized
1150   into deep learning and non-deep learning methods. Numerous non-neural network-based variants of the
1151   stochastic block model have been developed to model different notions of cluster structures (Peixoto, 2018;
1152
       2022), along with various inference algorithms (Peixoto, 2020; Mao & Zhang, 2023).
1153   Attributed stochastic block models include the contextual stochastic block model (Deshpande et al., 2018)
1154   and the neural-prior stochastic block model (Duranthon & Zdeborová, 2023b). The contextual stochastic
1155   block model generates both node attributes and graph structure from node clusters (see appendix A.1.2), and
1156   prior works have proposed various distributions to model the generation of node attributes and adjacencies
1157   from node clusters including the exponential family of distributions (Dreveton et al., 2023).
1158   Generative models of graphs with cluster structure that incorporate neural networks in their generative pro-
1159   cesses include the aforementioned neural-prior stochastic block model (see appendix A.1.1) and the feature-
1160   first block model (Tray & Kontoyiannis, 2021). The Feature-first Block Model (Tray & Kontoyiannis, 2021)
1161   is a similar model to the neural-prior stochastic block model (Duranthon & Zdeborová, 2023b) proposed with
1162   an inference algorithm proposed consisting a two-part Markov chain Monte Carlo approach with Metropolis
1163   Hastings.
1164   In this work we propose alternative fully neural variants of the contextual and neural stochastic block model
1165   and a corresponding deep learning-based inference framework that composes common neural network mod-
1166   ules, providing both a perspective to understand current graph neural network approaches for graph cluster-
1167   ing and an alternative deep learning approach for graph learning that enables graph learning with transform-
1168   ers and multilayer perceptrons.
1169
1170   D.3   S EMI -S UPERVISED D EEP G RAPH C LUSTERING
1171
1172   The application of the neural-prior stochastic block model and contextual stochastic block model to semi-
1173   supervised clustering been explored in synthetic data, inferred by an approximate message passing and belief
1174   propagation scheme (Duranthon & Zdeborová, 2023b;a). This work proposes a fully neural version of the


                                                        25
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1175
       contextual stochastic block model, and formulates the inference of the proposes NSBM and CSBM variant
1176
       as neural network learning with gradient descent.
1177
1178   Graph neural network approaches for semi-supervised clustering have been explored in He et al. (2022c;b;a).
1179   Leveraging a supervised clustering objective based on graph kernel methods (Tian et al., 2019), He et al.
1180
       (2022c) proposed a semi-supervised node clustering framework SSSNet with graph neural networks for
       signed graphs, later adapted for directed graphs He et al. (2022b;a)). SSSNet is trained with an unsupervised
1181
       clustering loss together with two supervised losses proposed by Tian et al. (2019): a supervised cross-entropy
1182
       classification loss and a third “triplet” graph kernel-based loss. DIGRAC (He et al., 2022b) and MSGNN
1183   He et al. (2022a) extended the framework for semi-supervised clustering on synthetic directed and signed
1184   directed graphs, respectively. DIGRAC studied on synthetic graphs generated from directed stochastic block
1185   models, without attributes as an ablation study, comparing self-supervised vs supervised methods. MSGNN
1186   evaluated various graph neural network architectures for directed and signed graphs using this three-part
1187   semi-supervised loss.
1188
       Our contributions are complementary, as we draw connections with other end-to-end graph clustering ap-
1189   proaches, propose a positional encoding-free learning setting for transformer and multilayer perceptron with-
1190   out graph neural network layers, and position semi-supervised clustering as inference of (neural) attributed
1191   graph generative models. While the frameworks are compatible in principle, we did not include the unsu-
1192   pervised graph clustering losses in He et al. (2022c) and He et al. (2022b) for the following reasons: The
1193   unsupervised clustering loss proposed in He et al. (2022c) was designed for distinguishing friends and ene-
1194   mies and is unsuitable for unsigned graphs (He et al., 2022c). The experiments were unable to incorporate
1195   the open source implementations of the unsupervised probabilistic imbalance clustering loss in DIGRAC
1196   (He et al., 2022b; 2023) due to memory issues.
1197   We refer the reader to Shukla et al. (2019) for a survey of semi-supervised graph clustering methods, includ-
1198   ing neural network-based methods. The survey highlights the graph neural networks for semi-supervised
1199   clustering as a promising direction for future research, and the proposed framework in this work is a step in
1200   this direction.
1201
1202
1203
       D.4   D EEP ATTRIBUTED G RAPH C LUSTERING
1204
1205
1206   Methods for deep attributed graph clustering can be categorized into end-to-end deep attributed graph clus-
1207
       tering methods and multi-step deep attributed graph clustering methods (Wang et al., 2023; Liu et al., 2023)
       End-to-end deep attributed graph clustering methods learn node embeddings and cluster them in a single
1208
       step, while multi-step deep attributed graph clustering methods first learn node embeddings and then cluster
1209
       them with a separate clustering method.
1210
1211   End-to-end deep attributed graph clustering methods include NOCD (Shchur & Günnemann, 2019), DMoN
1212   (Tsitsulin et al., 2023), and Neuromap (?).
1213   Multi-step deep attributed graph clustering methods include Veličković et al. (2019); Zhu et al. (2020);
1214   Hassani & Khasahmadi (2020); Thakoor et al. (2022); Devvrit et al. (2022) that focus on self-supervised
1215   representation learning that enables a two-step classification or clustering approach by first learning node
1216   embeddings and then classifying these embeddings with a separate classifier or clustering them using non-
1217   neural network clustering methods such as k-means.
1218   Our work focuses on end-to-end deep attributed graph clustering methods that learn node embeddings and
1219   cluster them in a single step. However, as our framework is compatible with any end-to-end unsupervised
1220   / self-supervised graph learning objective, it can in principle be used with end-to-end self-supervised graph
1221   representation learning methods. We leave the exploration of this for future work.


                                                        26
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1222
       We refer the reader to Wang et al. (2023) and Liu et al. (2023) and for recent overviews and surveys of deep
1223
       graph clustering methods.
1224
1225
       D.5   L EARNING ON G RAPHS WITH “P URE ” T RANSFORMERS AND MLP S
1226
1227   Transformers (Vaswani et al., 2017) are set-to-set and sequence-to-sequence (Yun et al., 2020) artificial
1228   neural networks that employ attention mechanisms to model relationships between input sets or sequences.
1229   Graph transformers (Rampasek et al., 2022) mix graph neural network layers with attention layers and
1230   use additional positional encodings informative of graph structure (Huang et al., 2024) to learn on graphs.
1231   Transformers without graph neural network layers can also learn on graphs using these positional encodings
1232   informative of graph structure (Ying et al., 2021; Kim et al., 2022; Ma et al., 2023). Conversely, Buterez
1233   et al. (2024) showed how Transformers without positional encodings can use adjacency matrices as attention
1234
       masks to learn on graphs effectively, an approach that can be interpreted as using graph attention (Veličković
       et al., 2018). In this work we propose a learning method for Transformers and MLPs that neither uses graph
1235
       neural network layers nor positional encodings.
1236
1237   Blöcker et al. (2024) and Shchur & Günnemann (2019) have evaluated the performance MLPs for unsu-
1238   pervised attributed graph clustering, equivalent to MLP-based NSBMs in our framework for the purely
1239   unsupervised setting. Although Shchur & Günnemann (2019) reported significantly poorer relative unsuper-
1240
       vised clustering performance of MLPs relative to GCNs when optimizing the NOCD clustering objective,
       the more recent evaluation by Blöcker et al. (2024) on more recent datasets (including datasets used in our
1241
       experiments) and methods demonstrated that MLPs perform similarly to GNNs with different clustering ob-
1242
       jectives. Notably the results by Blöcker et al. (2024) also demonstrated that while MLPs had significantly
1243   poorer performance versus GNNs optimizing the NOCD clustering objective on certain datasets, MLPs op-
1244   timizing other clustering objectives on those datasets perform similarly to GNNs. Our work provides a
1245   complementary perspective to understand why MLPs should perform similarly to GNNs when optimizing
1246   graph clustering objectives for attributed graph clustering, and introduces a framework for evaluating end-to-
1247   end graph clustering objectives on real-world attributed graphs in the semi-supervised setting. We propose
1248   that the variability of MLP performance due to different clustering objectives could be an interesting avenue
1249   for future work.
1250
1251   D.6   G RAPH AUTOENCODERS WITH ATTRIBUTE R ECONSTRUCTION
1252
1253
       While prototypical graph autoencoder (Kipf & Welling, 2016b) were designed to only reconstruct graph
       structure, a notable exception developed in later works is the family of graph autoencoders for deep graph
1254
       generation that includes GraphVAE (Simonovsky & Komodakis, 2018) that reconstruct both node attributes
1255
       and graph structure (Zhu et al., 2022). Wang et al. (2022) proposed and evaluated a specific graph autoen-
1256   coder architecture that reconstructs both node attributes and graph structure for attributed graph clustering
1257   and link prediction on the Planetoid datasets Cora, Citeseer, and Pubmed (Yang et al., 2016). Conversely,
1258   Hou et al. (2022) explored graph autoencoders that reconstruct attributes instead of graph structure for node
1259   classification and graph classification.
1260
       Our work introduces a perspective to understand the importance of reconstructing both node attributes and
1261
       graph structure for learning on attributed graphs with graph neural networks, and introduces a general frame-
1262   work that can utilize any graph autoencoder that reconstructs both node attributes and graph structure for
1263   semi-supervised graph clustering.
1264
1265
       D.7   C LUSTERING for G RAPH N EURAL N ETWORKS
1266
1267   Graph neural networks can be designed to implicitly and/or explicitly utilize graph cluster structures in
1268   their learning. Implicit optimization of clustering objectives by graph neural networks have been studied in


                                                         27
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1269
       works such as Hansen & Bianchi (2023) which studied how a local quadratic variation partitioning objective
1270
       is minimized by message passing graph neural networks such as graph convolutional networks (Kipf &
1271   Welling, 2016a).
1272
1273   Explicit uses of clustering objectives include pooling (Ying et al., 2018; Bianchi et al., 2020; Hansen &
1274
       Bianchi, 2023) and batch sampling (Chiang et al., 2019) for graph neural networks. Clustering-based pooling
       approaches in aforementioned works such as DiffPool (Ying et al., 2018), MinCutPool (Bianchi et al., 2020),
1275
       and TVGNN (Hansen & Bianchi, 2023) use explicit clustering objectives to pool graphs for graph-level tasks
1276
       such as graph classification and graph regression. Clustering-based subgraph methods include Chiang et al.
1277   (2019) that propose using low-memory complexity graph clustering algorithms suited to large graphs to
1278   sample and batch large graphs, reducing memory requirements for graph neural networks. Finkelshtein
1279   et al. (2024) studied a similar approach that leveraged community detection to sparsify large graphs.
1280
       Mehta et al. (2019) incorporated the stochastic block model into the design of variational graph autoencoders
1281
       (Kipf & Welling, 2016b) to model sparse graphs. Yang et al. (2020) study a connection between graph
1282
       attention networks (Veličković et al., 2018) and the stochastic block model, framing graph attention networks
1283   as semi-amortized variational inference of stochastic block models. Our work provides a complementary
1284   perspective to understand how neural networks can infer attributed stochastic block models. We propose end-
1285   to-end fully neural variants of attributed stochastic block models that can be used with any neural network
1286   architecture. Our framework describes how any graph neural network can infer these attributed stochastic
1287   block models with attribute reconstruction, and how non-graph neural networks can learn on attributed
1288   graphs without positional encodings or graph neural network layers such as message passing or spectral
1289   convolutions by inferring these attributed stochastic block models.
1290

       the experiments in this work.

1297   E.1   DATASET D ETAILS
1298
1299   We sourced the real-world attributed graph multi-class node classification datasets in our experiments from
1300
       the following code and data repositories using the following libraries.
1301
              • Coauthor CS, Coauthor Physics, Amazon Computer, and Amazon Photo from Shchur et al. (2018)
1302
                using the geometric deep learning library PyTorch Geometric (Fey & Lenssen, 2019).
1303
1304          • roman-empire and amazon-ratings from Platonov et al. (2023b) using PyTorch Geometric.
1305
1306   Each undirected edge between nodes i and j in the undirected graph datasets is represented with two directed
1307
       edges, one from node i to j and another from node j to i, following the approach in PyTorch Geometric (Fey
       & Lenssen, 2019). Node attributes are normalized so that the attributes for each node sum to 1.
1308
1309   10 splits for each dataset roman-empire and amazon-ratings from Platonov et al. (2023b) were
1310   made public. 1 split for each dataset ogbn-arxiv and ogbn-products from the Open Graph Bench-
1311   mark (Hu et al., 2021) was made public. The splits for the Coauthor CS, Coauthor Physics, Amazon Com-
1312   puter, and Amazon Photo datasets from Shchur et al. (2018) were not made public, but the work proposes
1313
       10 random splits of 20 training nodes per class, 500 validation nodes, and 1000 test nodes.
1314   We create a label-sparsified version of each dataset by selecting 2 training nodes per class and 50 validation
1315   nodes from the splits described above. We do not modify the test labels.

                                                         28
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1316
       We run each experiment configuration 10 times on each dataset and its label-sparsified version and report
1317
       the mean and standard deviation of the results. For the roman-empire and amazon-ratings datasets,
1318   we use a different public split for each run. For the Coauthor CS, Coauthor Physics, Amazon Computer, and
1319   Amazon Photo datasets we use a random split with the same number of training nodes per class, validation
1320   nodes, and test nodes for each run.

1337   E.2   A RCHITECTURE D ETAILS
1338
1339   The transformer architecture used is a prototypical 6 layer encoder-only transformer (Vaswani et al., 2017),
1340
       adopting its default PyTorch (Ansel et al., 2024) with 8 attention heads, 512 embedding dimensions and
       2048 hidden dimensions in its feedforward block, with a bias in its linear and layernorm layers. MLP is
1341
       equivalent to the feedforward block in the transformer. Both use a dropout rate of 0.1.
1342
1343   GCN and GraphSAGE are the graph neural network architectures used in experiments, adopting its imple-
1344   mentations in PyTorch Geometric (Fey & Lenssen, 2019). They use 3 layers, 256 hidden dimensions, and a
1345   dropout rate of 0.5.
1346   All models were trained with s = 100 cluster dimensions. This was chosen to be of the same order of
1347   magnitude as the square root of the number of nodes of the median dataset size, following empirical studies
1348   of the upper bound of the number of clusters in real-world graphs (Ghasemian et al., 2019). As graph
1349
                A
       structure    is reconstructed from S, the number of clusters s is chosen to be large enough so that A =
                                           b                                                                  b
1350   SBMf S is not too lossy a reconstruction of A, while being small enough to limit the computational
                 b
1351
       complexity of the model.
1352
1353   As evidenced by the additional experimental results in section F, having the number of clusters s larger than
1354   the number of classes does not significantly affect the performance of a purely supervised model.
1355
1356
       E.3   H YPERPARAMETER S ETTINGS
1357
1358   For a controlled experiment under a limited compute budget, hyperparameters such as learning rates or
1359   the number of layers were not tuned. Architectural hyperparameters are detailed above in the architecture
1360   details. We used the same learning rate of 0.01 for all parameters, for all experiments, optimized with
1361   AdamW (Loshchilov & Hutter, 2019). Where Lregularization is “L2” weight decay is set to 1.0 and otherwise
1362   weight decay is set to 0.01.

                                                        29
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1363
       E.4     U NSUPERVISED C LUSTERING O BJECTIVES
1364
1365   In this section we provide additional details on the unsupervised clustering objectives used in the experi-
1366   ments.
1367
1368   E.4.1    LE O BJECTIVES
1369
1370
       We evaluated 4 unsupervised clustering objectives – NOCD (Shchur & Günnemann, 2019), DMoN (Tsitsulin
       et al., 2023), Neuromap (Blöcker et al., 2024), and our proposed SBMNN – as c ≈ LE objectives (see section
1371
       2.1.1) in the experiments.
1372
1373   As NOCD can be interpreted as a special case of the SBM (see section A), we implement NOCD simply as
1374   the case where the SBM has a fixed identity block matrix B = I. Instead of the custom activation function
1375   in the NOCD implementation to generate valid edge probabilities, we use the sigmoid activation function
1376
       which matches a typical binary cross entropy from logits loss used in link prediction objectives (Zhang &
       Chen, 2018; Li et al., 2023; Qarkaxhija et al., 2024). We argue that these implementations are equivalent.
1377
1378   “L2” is l2 regularization on the weights of a neural network, motivated by the interpretation of these neural
1379   networks as generative models (Graves, 2011).
1380   DMoN comprises two unsupervised objectives – a spectral modularity objective and a “collapse” regulariza-
1381   tion objective. We use its spectral modularity objective as an LE objectives and its collapse regularization
1382   objective as an Lregularization objective. We chose DMoN’s objectives as representative LE and Lregularization
1383   objectives as they were designed to jointly clustering node attributes and adjacencies (unsupervised) using
1384   graph neural networks.
1385
       DMoN’s spectral modularity LE objective is calculated as
1386
                                                      1                 d⊗d
1387                                  (LE )DMoN := −    Tr S T A −              S                              (20)
1388                                                 2m                  2m
1389   where m = |E| is the number of edges in the graph and d is a degree vector representing the sum of the rows
1390   of the adjacency matrix A, and d ⊗ d is the outer product of d with itself. The implementation of DMoN
1391   (Tsitsulin et al., 2023) was sourced from PyTorch Geometric (Fey & Lenssen, 2019).
1392   Neurommap’s (Blöcker et al., 2024) codelength LE objective is calculated as
1393

       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1410
       and the vector p of node visit rates is calculated using smart teleportation Lambiotte & Rosvall (2012) and
1411
       the power iteration method which iteratively updates the visit rates p(t) (until convergence) as
1412
1413                                                     α in
                                             p(t+1) ←        d + (1 − α) p(t) T                                     (24)
1414                                                    wtot
1415
       where p(0) = din and α is a parameter chosen proportionally to the nodes’ in-degrees. The implementation
1416
       of Neuromap (Blöcker et al., 2024) was sourced from its official repository at https://github.com/
1417
       chrisbloecker/neuromap.
1418
1419   For the SBMNN we propose, we evaluated two neural networks as NNS→ b Z b in equation 4 to generate B –
1420   a transformer and an MLP with the same architectures and hyperparameter settings as the transformers and
1421
       MLPs detailed above.
1422
1423   E.4.2    LV OBJECTIVES
1424   For the “GCN” and “GraphSAGE” models that refer to CBAE autoencoders inferring a CSBM according to
1425   equation 7 we evaluated two choices of NNS→ b X b – a transformer and an MLP. We set the transformer and
1426   MLP architectures and hyperparameter settings the same as the transformers and MLPs detailed above. LV
1427   was then calculated as the mean squared error of the reconstruction X
                                                                           c of X.
1428
1429   E.4.3    R EGULARIZATION OBJECTIVES
1430
1431   We evaluated two Lregularization objectives – “L2” and “DMoN” (Tsitsulin et al., 2023).
1432
       The collapse regularization Lregularization objective of DMoN aims to discourage the trivial partition of assign-
1433   ing all nodes to the same cluster and can be expressed as
1434
                                                                  √ X
1435                                                               s
1436                                    (Lregularization )DMoN :=       (S:,i ) − 1                                 (25)
                                                                  n   i
1437                                                                             2

1438   where ∥·∥2 is the l2 vector norm. The implementation of DMoN (Tsitsulin et al., 2023) was sourced from
1439   PyTorch Geometric (Fey & Lenssen, 2019).
1440
       L2 regularization was calculated as the l2 norm of all weights in the neural network. Shchur & Günnemann
1441
       (2019) showed that small L2 regularization was beneficial, and we evaluate the case of unit L2 regularization
1442
       which corresponds to an exact variational and description length objective (Graves, 2011), motivated by the
1443   interpretation of these model as generative models (see section B.2.3).
1444
1445
       E.5     E VALUATION M ETRICS
1446
1447   The experiments focused on semi-supervised multi-class node classification for real-world node-attributed
1448   graphs. Two evaluation metrics are used: accuracy and Matthews correlation coefficient (Gösgens et al.,
1449   2021; Platonov et al., 2023a). Both metrics are evaluated on the test set for each dataset and split. Accuracy
1450   alone is often standard as a sole evaluation metric for multi-class classification (as done in Yang et al. (2016);
1451   Kipf & Welling (2016a); Shchur et al. (2018); Platonov et al. (2023b); Hu et al. (2021)).
1452   We also employed the Matthews correlation coefficient as an additional evaluation metric to provide a more
1453   comprehensive evaluation due to its theoretical and empirical (Gösgens et al., 2021; Platonov et al., 2023a)
1454   advantages in evaluating classifications, and its use in graph learning with encoding-free transformers with-
1455   out graph neural network layers such as Buterez et al. (2024). Its advantages are evident in the experiments,
1456   such as in evaluating performance on the amazon-ratings dataset (see table 1). Despite some models

                                                          31
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1457
       achieving an accuracy of roughly 0.3 and other models achieving near zero accuracy, the Matthews corre-
1458
       lation coefficient was zero across most models, indicating that these models were not predicting any better
1459   than random.
1460
1461   Other clustering evaluation metrics such as modularity or graph conductance (as used in Tsitsulin et al.
1462
       (2023) for unsupervised clustering) were not used in the experiments as they are defined for non-attributed
       graphs.
1463
1464
1465
1466
1467
1468
1469
1470
1471
       E.6   E ARLY S TOPPING AND M ODEL S ELECTION WITHOUT A VALIDATION S ET
1472
1473
1474
1475
1476   While early stopping and model selection on the validation set is standard practice for supervised learning
1477   (Li et al., 2020; Bai et al., 2021; Ji et al., 2021), for unsupervised learning a validation set is unvailable, and
1478   early stopping and model selection on the training loss has been explored for unsupervised graph clustering
1479
       (Shchur & Günnemann, 2019; Blöcker et al., 2024). As our framework positions semi-supervised clustering
       as from both supervised and unsupervised clustering perspectives, we evaluated early stopping and model
1480
       selection on the training loss and on the validation Matthews correlation coefficient (MCC). Despite the small
1481
       validation set size (50 nodes) on label sparsified splits, we found that early stopping and model selection on
1482   the validation MCC produced the best generalization performance on the test set for both default and label
1483   sparsified splits of the datasets.
1484
1485
1486
1487
1488
1489
1490
1491
1492   E.7   S OFTWARE U SED
1493
1494
1495
1496
       Experiments leveraged PyTorch (Ansel et al., 2024) and PyTorch Geometric (PyG) (Fey & Lenssen, 2019)
1497
       libraries for graph deep learning, and scikit learn for evaluation metrics (Pedregosa et al., 2011). Compiling
1498
       results into tables utilized the library pandas (The pandas development team)., and visualizations utilized
1499   the libraries cuGraph (Team, 2023), NetworkX (Hagberg et al., 2008), and Matplotlib (Hunter, 2007). In
1500   particular, visualizations used the ForceAtlas2 (Jacomy et al., 2014) algorithm implementation by the GPU
1501   accelerated cuGraph library. All experiments were run on a SLURM (Yoo et al., 2003) cluster with Apptainer
1502   (formerly Singularity) (Kurtzer et al., 2017) using the PyG container NVIDIA NGC container registry which
1503   contains all of the software dependencies above.

3184
3185   F.4     C OMPARISON OF P OOLING M ETHODS AND R EGULARISATION
3186
3187   In this section we present results comparing graph clustering and regularization objectives and the effect of
3188   ablating regularization objectives. We compare clustering with regularization, clustering without regulariza-
3189
       tion, and regularization without clustering for each neural network.
3190   Our results show that unsupervised graph clustering objectives with regularization outperform regularization
3191   alone, ablating the effect of regularization.
3192
